1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/%D0%A1%D1%82%D0%B5%D0%BB%D0%B0_%D0%BD%D0%B0%D1%87%D0%B0%D0%BB%D1%8C%D0%BD%D0%B8%D0%BA%D0%B0_%D0%B3%D0%BE%D0%BD%D1%87%D0%B0%D1%80%D0%BE%D0%B2_%D0%9F%D0%B5%D0%BF%D0%B8.jpg/284px-%D0%A1%D1%82%D0%B5%D0%BB%D0%B0_%D0%BD%D0%B0%D1%87%D0%B0%D0%BB%D1%8C%D0%BD%D0%B8%D0%BA%D0%B0_%D0%B3%D0%BE%D0%BD%D1%87%D0%B0%D1%80%D0%BE%D0%B2_%D0%9F%D0%B5%D0%BF%D0%B8.jpg 2x" width="142"/></a></div></div></div><div class="trow" style="display:flex"><div class="thumbcaption">Various examples of calligraphy in different <a href="/wiki/Language" title="Language">languages</a> and <a href="/wiki/Writing_system" title="Writing system">writing systems</a> throughout history</div></div></div></div>
<p><b>Calligraphy</b> (from <a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">Greek</a>: <span lang="el">καλλιγραφία</span>) is a <a href="/wiki/Visual_arts" title="Visual arts">visual art</a> related to <a href="/wiki/Writing" title="Writing">writing</a>. It is the design and execution of <a href="/wiki/Lettering" title="Lettering">lettering</a> with a <a href="/wiki/Pen" title="Pen">pen</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ink_brush" title="Ink brush">ink brush</a>, or other writing instrument.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mediaville1996_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-mediaville1996-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 17">: 17 </span></sup> A contemporary calligraphic practice can be defined as "the art of giving form to signs in an expressive, harmonious, and skillful manner".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mediaville1996_1-1"><a href="#cite_note-mediaville1996-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 18">: 18 </span></sup>
</p><p>Modern calligraphy ranges from functional inscriptions and designs to fine-art pieces where the letters may or may not be readable.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mediaville1996_1-2"><a href="#cite_note-mediaville1996-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title="This citation requires a reference to the specific page or range of pages in which the material appears. (August 2016)">page needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Classical calligraphy differs from <a href="/wiki/Type_design" title="Type design">type design</a> and non-classical hand-lettering, though a calligrapher may practice both.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEZapf2007_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEZapf2007-4">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-zapf2006_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-zapf2006-5">[5]</a></sup>
</p><p>Calligraphy continues to flourish in the forms of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wedding_invitations" title="Wedding invitations">wedding invitations</a> and event invitations, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Font_design" title="Font design">font design</a> and <a href="/wiki/Typography" title="Typography">typography</a>, original hand-lettered <a href="/wiki/Logo" title="Logo">logo</a> design, <a href="/wiki/Religious_art" title="Religious art">religious art</a>, announcements, <a href="/wiki/Graphic_design" title="Graphic design">graphic design</a> and commissioned calligraphic art, cut stone <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Inscription" title="Inscription">inscriptions</a>, and memorial <a href="/wiki/Document" title="Document">documents</a>. It is also used for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Theatrical_property" title="Theatrical property">props</a> and moving images for film and television, and also for <a href="/wiki/Testimonial" title="Testimonial">testimonials</a>, <a href="/wiki/Birth_certificate" title="Birth certificate">birth</a> and <a href="/wiki/Death_certificate" title="Death certificate">death certificates</a>, <a href="/wiki/Map" title="Map">maps</a>, and other written works.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup>
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r886046785">.mw-parser-output .toclimit-2 .toclevel-1 ul,.mw-parser-output .toclimit-3 .toclevel-2 ul,.mw-parser-output .toclimit-4 .toclevel-3 ul,.mw-parser-output .toclimit-5 .toclevel-4 ul,.mw-parser-output .toclimit-6 .toclevel-5 ul,.mw-parser-output .toclimit-7 .toclevel-6 ul{display:none}</style><div class="toclimit-3"><div aria-labelledby="mw-toc-heading" class="toc" id="toc" role="navigation"><input class="toctogglecheckbox" id="toctogglecheckbox" role="button" style="display:none" type="checkbox"/><div class="toctitle" dir="ltr" lang="en"><h2 id="mw-toc-heading">Contents</h2><span class="toctogglespan"><label class="toctogglelabel" for="toctogglecheckbox"></label></span></div>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Tools">Tools</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Calligraphy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Tools">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Pens_and_brushes">Pens and brushes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Calligraphy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Pens and brushes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1096954695/mw-parser-output/.tmulti" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner multiimageinner" style="width:322px;max-width:322px"><div class="trow"><div class="tsingle" style="width:184px;max-width:184px"><div class="thumbimage" style="height:182px;overflow:hidden"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Pointed_pen_parts.svg"><img alt="" data-file-height="340" data-file-width="340" decoding="async" height="182" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/Pointed_pen_parts.svg/182px-Pointed_pen_parts.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/Pointed_pen_parts.svg/273px-Pointed_pen_parts.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/Pointed_pen_parts.svg/364px-Pointed_pen_parts.svg.png 2x" width="182"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption">DA calligraphic <a href="/wiki/Nib_(pen)" title="Nib (pen)">nib</a>, with part names</div></div><div class="tsingle" style="width:134px;max-width:134px"><div class="thumbimage" style="height:182px;overflow:hidden"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Upper_Lascar_Row,_Hong_Kong_(2051987459).jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="3071" data-file-width="2221" decoding="async" height="183" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Upper_Lascar_Row%2C_Hong_Kong_%282051987459%29.jpg/132px-Upper_Lascar_Row%2C_Hong_Kong_%282051987459%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Upper_Lascar_Row%2C_Hong_Kong_%282051987459%29.jpg/198px-Upper_Lascar_Row%2C_Hong_Kong_%282051987459%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Upper_Lascar_Row%2C_Hong_Kong_%282051987459%29.jpg/264px-Upper_Lascar_Row%2C_Hong_Kong_%282051987459%29.jpg 2x" width="132"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption">Ink brushes of various size and material</div></div></div></div></div>
<p>The principal tools for a calligrapher are the <a href="/wiki/Pen" title="Pen">pen</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Paintbrush" title="Paintbrush">brush</a>. Calligraphy pens write with <a href="/wiki/Nib_(pen)" title="Nib (pen)">nibs</a> that may be flat, round, or pointed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup> For some decorative purposes, multi-nibbed pens—steel brushes—can be used. However, works have also been created with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Felt-tip_pen" title="Felt-tip pen">felt-tip</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ballpoint_pen" title="Ballpoint pen">ballpoint pens</a>, although these works do not employ angled lines. There are some styles of calligraphy, such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gothic_(script)" title="Gothic (script)">Gothic script</a>, that require a stub nib pen.
</p><p>Common calligraphy pens and brushes are:
</p>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Quill" title="Quill">Quill</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Dip_pen" title="Dip pen">Dip pen</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Ink_brush" title="Ink brush">Ink brush</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Qalam" title="Qalam">Qalam</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Fountain_pen" title="Fountain pen">Fountain pen</a></li>
<li>Chiselled marker</li></ul>
<h3><span id="Inks.2C_papers.2C_and_templates"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Inks,_papers,_and_templates">Inks, papers, and templates</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Calligraphy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Inks, papers, and templates">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Writing ink is usually water-based and is much less viscous than the oil-based inks used in printing. Certain specialty paper with high ink absorption and constant texture enables cleaner lines,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup> although <a href="/wiki/Parchment" title="Parchment">parchment</a> or <a href="/wiki/Vellum" title="Vellum">vellum</a> is often used, as a knife can be used to erase imperfections and a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Light-box" title="Light-box">light-box</a> is not needed to allow lines to pass through it. Normally, light boxes and templates are used to achieve straight lines without pencil markings detracting from the work. Ruled paper, either for a light box or direct use, is most often ruled every quarter or half inch, although inch spaces are occasionally used. This is the case with <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">litterea unciales</i></span> (hence the name), and college-<a href="/wiki/Ruled_paper" title="Ruled paper">ruled paper</a> often acts as a guideline well.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="World_traditions">World traditions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Calligraphy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: World traditions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="East_Asia_and_Vietnam">East Asia and Vietnam</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Calligraphy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: East Asia and Vietnam">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><span class="anchor" id="East_Asian_calligraphy"></span>
</p>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/East_Asian_cultural_sphere" title="East Asian cultural sphere">East Asian cultural sphere</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Chinese_calligraphy" title="Chinese calligraphy">Chinese calligraphy</a> is locally called <i><span title="ISO 7098 Chinese (Chinese language) transliteration"><i lang="zh-Latn">shūfǎ</i></span></i> or <i><span title="ISO 7098 Chinese (Chinese language) transliteration"><i lang="zh-Latn">fǎshū</i></span></i> (<span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">書法</span></span> or <span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">法書</span></span> in traditional Chinese, literally "the method or law of writing");<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[a]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Japanese_calligraphy" title="Japanese calligraphy">Japanese calligraphy</a> is <i><span title="Japanese-language romanization"><i lang="ja-Latn">shodō</i></span></i> (<span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">書道</span></span>, literally "the way or principle of writing");<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[13]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Korean_calligraphy" title="Korean calligraphy">Korean calligraphy</a> is called <i><span title="Korean-language romanization"><i lang="ko-Latn">seoye</i></span></i> (<a href="/wiki/Korean_language" title="Korean language">Korean</a>: <span lang="ko">서예</span>/<span title="Korean-language text"><span lang="ko-Hant">書藝</span></span>, literally "the art of writing");<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[14]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Vietnamese_calligraphy" title="Vietnamese calligraphy">Vietnamese calligraphy</a> is called <span title="Vietnamese-language text"><i lang="vi">thư pháp</i></span> (<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1051880798">.mw-parser-output .vi-nom{font-family:"Han-Nom Gothic","Nom Na Tong","Han-Nom Ming","Han-Nom Minh","HAN NOM A","HAN NOM B","Han-Nom Kai","TH-Khaai-TP0","TH-Khaai-TP2","TH-Khaai-PP0","TH-Khaai-PP2","TH-Sung-PP0","TH-Sung-PP2","TH-Sung-TP0","TH-Sung-TP2","TH-Sy-P0","TH-Sy-P2","TH-Ming-JP0","TH-Ming-JP2","Sun-ExtA","Sun-ExtB","Ming-Lt-HKSCS-UNI-H","Ming-Lt-HKSCS-ExtB","HanaMinA","HanaMinB","HanaMin","MingLiU","MingLiU-ExtB","MingLiU HKSCS","MingLiU HKSCS-ExtB","SimSun","SimSun-ExtB","FZKaiT-Extended","FZKaiT-Extended(SIP)",sans-serif}.mw-parser-output .vi-nom.ext{font-family:"Han-Nom Gothic","Han-Nom Ming","Han-Nom Minh","TH-Khaai-TP2","TH-Khaai-PP2","TH-Sung-PP2","TH-Sung-TP2","TH-Sy-P0","TH-Sy-P2","TH-Ming-JP2","BabelStone Han","Sun-ExtB","MingLiU HKSCS-ExtB","Ming-Lt-HKSCS-ExtB","HanaMinB","Han-Nom Kai",sans-serif}</style><span class="vi-nom" lang="vi-Hani">書法</span>, literally "the way of letters or words"). The calligraphy of <a href="/wiki/CJK_characters" title="CJK characters">East Asian characters</a> is an important and appreciated aspect of traditional <a href="/wiki/East_Asian_cultural_sphere" title="East Asian cultural sphere">East Asian culture</a>.
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1021810730">@media all and (max-width:720px){.mw-parser-output .mod-gallery{width:100%!important}}.mw-parser-output .mod-gallery{display:table}.mw-parser-output .mod-gallery-default{background:transparent;margin-top:0.3em}.mw-parser-output .mod-gallery-center{margin-left:auto;margin-right:auto}.mw-parser-output .mod-gallery-left{float:left}.mw-parser-output .mod-gallery-right{float:right}.mw-parser-output .mod-gallery-none{float:none}.mw-parser-output .mod-gallery-collapsible{width:100%}.mw-parser-output .mod-gallery .title,.mw-parser-output .mod-gallery .main,.mw-parser-output .mod-gallery .footer{display:table-row}.mw-parser-output .mod-gallery .title>div{display:table-cell;padding:0.2em 0 0.6em 1.6em;text-align:center;font-weight:bold}.mw-parser-output .mod-gallery .main>div{display:table-cell}.mw-parser-output .mod-gallery .gallery{line-height:1.35em}.mw-parser-output .mod-gallery .footer>div{display:table-cell;padding:0.2em 0 0.6em 1.6em;text-align:right;font-size:80%;line-height:1em}.mw-parser-output .mod-gallery .title>div *,.mw-parser-output .mod-gallery .footer>div *{overflow:visible}.mw-parser-output .mod-gallery .gallerybox img{background:none!important}.mw-parser-output .mod-gallery .bordered-images .thumb img{border:solid #eaecf0 1px}.mw-parser-output .mod-gallery .whitebg .thumb{background:#fff!important}</style><div class="mod-gallery mod-gallery-default"><div class="title"><div>Caligraphy samples from East Asia and Vietnam</div></div><div class="main"><div><ul class="gallery mw-gallery-traditional nochecker bordered-images whitebg" style="max-width: 446px;">

<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Calligraphy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>In <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ancient_China" title="Ancient China">ancient China</a>, the oldest known Chinese characters are <a href="/wiki/Oracle_bone_script" title="Oracle bone script">oracle bone script</a> (<span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">甲骨文</span></span>), carved on ox <a href="/wiki/Scapula" title="Scapula">scapulae</a> and tortoise <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Plastrons" title="Plastrons">plastrons</a>, because the rulers in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shang_Dynasty" title="Shang Dynasty">Shang Dynasty</a> carved pits on such animals' bones and then baked them to gain auspice of military affairs, agricultural harvest, or even procreating and weather.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="Awkward sentence (August 2021)">clarification needed</span></a></i>]</sup> During the <a href="/wiki/Divination" title="Divination">divination</a> ceremony, after the cracks were made, the characters were written with a brush on the shell or bone to be later carved.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[15]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources#What_information_to_include" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title="A complete citation is needed. (August 2022)">full citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> With the development of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bronzeware_script" title="Bronzeware script">Bronzeware script</a> (<span title="Chinese-language romanization"><i lang="zh-Latn">jīn wén</i></span>) and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Large_Seal_script" title="Large Seal script">Large Seal script</a> (<span title="Chinese-language romanization"><i lang="zh-Latn">dà zhuàn</i></span>)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[16]</a></sup> "cursive" signs continued. <a href="/wiki/Mao_Gong_ding" title="Mao Gong ding">Mao Gong Ding</a> is one of the most famous and typical Bronzeware scripts in Chinese calligraphic history. It has 500 characters on the bronze which is the largest number of bronze inscription we have discovered so far.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="Awkward sentence (August 2021)">clarification needed</span></a></i>]</sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[17]</a></sup> Moreover, each archaic kingdom of current China had its own set of characters.
</p><p>In <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Imperial_era_of_Chinese_history" title="Imperial era of Chinese history">Imperial China</a>, the graphs on old <a href="/wiki/Stele" title="Stele">steles</a>—some dating from 200 BCE, and in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Small_Seal_script" title="Small Seal script">Small Seal script</a> (<span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">小篆</span></span> <span title="Chinese-language romanization"><i lang="zh-Latn">xiǎo zhuàn</i></span>) style—are still <span class="clarify-content" style="padding-left:0.1em; padding-right:0.1em; color:#595959; border:1px solid #DDD;">accessible</span><sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Clarify" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title='Does this mean "readable"? (August 2021)'>clarify</span></a></i>]</sup>.
</p><p>About 220 BCE, the emperor <a href="/wiki/Qin_Shi_Huang" title="Qin Shi Huang">Qin Shi Huang</a>, the first to conquer the entire Chinese basin, imposed several reforms, among them <a href="/wiki/Li_Si" title="Li Si">Li Si</a>'s character unification, which created a set of 3300 standardized Small Seal characters.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[18]</a></sup> Despite the fact that the main writing implement of the time was already the brush, few papers survive from this period, and the main examples of this style are on steles.
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Clerical_script" title="Clerical script">Clerical script</a> (<span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh-TW">隸書</span></span>/<span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">隸书</span></span>) (<span title="Chinese-language romanization"><i lang="zh-Latn">lì shū</i></span>) which is more regularized, and in some ways similar to modern text, were also authorised under Qin Shi Huangdi.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Blakney,_p6_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-Blakney,_p6-20">[19]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability#Self-published_sources" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability"><span title="The material near this tag may rely on a self-published source. (February 2020)">self-published source?</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>Between clerical script and traditional regular script, there is another transitional type of calligraphic work called <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Wei_Bei&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Wei Bei (page does not exist)">Wei Bei</a>. It started during the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/North_and_South_dynasties" title="North and South dynasties">North and South dynasties</a> (420 to 589 CE) and ended before the <a href="/wiki/Tang_dynasty" title="Tang dynasty">Tang Dynasty</a> (618–907).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[20]</a></sup>
</p><p>Traditional <a href="/wiki/Regular_script" title="Regular script">regular script</a> (<span title="Chinese-language romanization"><i lang="zh-Latn">kǎi shū</i></span>), still in use today, and attributed to <a href="/wiki/Wang_Xizhi" title="Wang Xizhi">Wang Xizhi</a> (<span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">王羲之</span></span>, 303–361) and his followers, is even more regularized.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Blakney,_p6_20-1"><a href="#cite_note-Blakney,_p6-20">[19]</a></sup> Its spread was encouraged by <a href="/wiki/Li_Siyuan" title="Li Siyuan">Emperor Mingzong of Later Tang</a> (926–933), who ordered the <a href="/wiki/Woodblock_printing" title="Woodblock printing">printing</a> of the classics using new wooden blocks in Kaishu. Printing technologies here allowed a shape stabilization. The Kaishu shape of characters 1000 years ago was mostly similar to that at the end of Imperial China.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Blakney,_p6_20-2"><a href="#cite_note-Blakney,_p6-20">[19]</a></sup> But small changes have been made, for example in the shape of <big><span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">广</span></span></big> which is not absolutely the same in the <a href="/wiki/Kangxi_Dictionary" title="Kangxi Dictionary">Kangxi Dictionary</a> of 1716 as in modern books. The Kangxi and current shapes have tiny differences, while stroke order is still the same, according to the old style.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[21]</a></sup>
</p><p>Styles which did not survive include <span title="Chinese-language romanization"><i lang="zh-Latn">bāfēnshū</i></span>, a mix of 80% Small Seal script and 20% Clerical script.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Blakney,_p6_20-3"><a href="#cite_note-Blakney,_p6-20">[19]</a></sup> Some <a href="/wiki/Variant_Chinese_characters" title="Variant Chinese characters">variant Chinese characters</a> were unorthodox or locally used for centuries. They were generally understood but always rejected in official texts. Some of these unorthodox variants, in addition to some newly created characters, compose the <a href="/wiki/Simplified_Chinese_characters" title="Simplified Chinese characters">simplified Chinese</a> character set.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Technique">Technique</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Calligraphy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Technique">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Traditional East Asian writing uses the <a href="/wiki/Four_Treasures_of_the_Study" title="Four Treasures of the Study">Four Treasures of the Study</a> (<span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh-TW">文房四寶</span></span>/<span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">文房四宝</span></span>):<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[22]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Ink_brush" title="Ink brush">ink brushes</a> known as <span title="Chinese-language romanization"><i lang="zh-Latn">máobǐ</i></span> (<span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh-TW">毛筆</span></span>/<span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">毛笔</span></span>), <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chinese_ink" title="Chinese ink">Chinese ink</a>, paper, and <a href="/wiki/Inkstone" title="Inkstone">inkstones</a> to write <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chinese_character" title="Chinese character">Chinese characters</a>.  These instruments of writing are also known as the <i>Four Friends of the Study</i> (<a href="/wiki/Korean_language" title="Korean language">Korean</a>: <span lang="ko">문방사우/文房四友</span>, <small><a href="/wiki/Romanization_of_Korean" title="Romanization of Korean">romanized</a>: </small><span title="Korean-language romanization"><i lang="ko-Latn">Munbang sau</i></span>) in Korea. Besides the traditional four tools, <a href="/wiki/Desk_pad" title="Desk pad">desk pads</a> and paperweights are also used.
</p><p>Many different parameters influence the final result of a calligrapher's work. Physical parameters include the shape, size, stretch, and <a href="/wiki/Brush#Bristles" title="Brush">hair type</a> of the ink brush; the color, color density and water density of the ink; as well as the paper's water absorption speed and surface texture. The calligrapher's technique also influences the result, as the look of finished characters are influenced by the quantity of ink and water the calligrapher lets the brush take and by the pressure, inclination, and direction of the brush. Changing these variables produces thinner or bolder strokes, and smooth or toothed borders. Eventually, the speed, accelerations and decelerations of a skilled calligrapher's movements aim to give "spirit" to the characters, greatly influencing their final shapes.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Styles">Styles</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Calligraphy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Styles">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Cursive styles such as <i><a href="/wiki/Semi-cursive_script" title="Semi-cursive script"><span title="Chinese-language romanization"><i lang="zh-Latn">xíngshū</i></span></a></i> (<span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh-TW">行書</span></span>/<span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">行书</span></span>)(semi-cursive or running script) and <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Grass_script" title="Grass script"><span title="Chinese-language romanization"><i lang="zh-Latn">cǎoshū</i></span></a></i> (<span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh-TW">草書</span></span>/<span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">草书</span></span>)(cursive, rough script, or grass script) are less constrained and faster, where more movements made by the writing implement are visible. These styles' stroke orders vary more, sometimes creating radically different forms. They are descended from Clerical script, in the same time as Regular script (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Han_Dynasty" title="Han Dynasty">Han Dynasty</a>), but <i><span title="Chinese-language romanization"><i lang="zh-Latn">xíngshū</i></span></i> and <i><span title="Chinese-language romanization"><i lang="zh-Latn">cǎoshū</i></span></i> were used for personal notes only, and never used as a standard. The <i><span title="Chinese-language romanization"><i lang="zh-Latn">cǎoshū</i></span></i>  style was highly appreciated in <a href="/wiki/Emperor_Wu_of_Han" title="Emperor Wu of Han">Emperor Wu of Han</a> reign (140–187 CE).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Blakney,_p6_20-4"><a href="#cite_note-Blakney,_p6-20">[19]</a></sup>
</p><p>Examples of modern printed styles are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ming_(typeface)" title="Ming (typeface)">Song</a> from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Song_Dynasty" title="Song Dynasty">Song Dynasty</a>'s <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Four_Great_Inventions_of_ancient_China#Printing" title="Four Great Inventions of ancient China">printing press</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/East_Asian_sans-serif_typeface" title="East Asian sans-serif typeface">sans-serif</a>. These are not considered traditional styles, and are normally not written.
</p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Influences">Influences</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Calligraphy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Influences">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Japanese, Korean and Vietnamese calligraphy were each greatly influenced by Chinese calligraphy. Calligraphy has also influenced <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ink_and_wash_painting" title="Ink and wash painting">ink and wash painting</a>, which is accomplished using similar tools and techniques. Calligraphy has influenced most major art styles in East Asia, including <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ink_and_wash_painting" title="Ink and wash painting">ink and wash painting</a>, a style of <a href="/wiki/Chinese_painting" title="Chinese painting">Chinese</a>, <a href="/wiki/Japanese_painting" title="Japanese painting">Japanese</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Korean_painting" title="Korean painting">Korean</a> based entirely on calligraphy.
</p><p>The Japanese, Korean and Vietnamese people have also developed their own specific sensibilities and styles of calligraphy while incorporating Chinese influences.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Japan">Japan</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Calligraphy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Japan">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Japanese_calligraphy" title="Japanese calligraphy">Japanese calligraphy</a></div>
<p>Japanese calligraphy goes out of the set of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/CJK_strokes" title="CJK strokes">CJK strokes</a> to also include local alphabets such as <a href="/wiki/Hiragana" title="Hiragana">hiragana</a> and <a href="/wiki/Katakana" title="Katakana">katakana</a>, with specific problematics such as new curves and moves, and specific materials (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Japanese_paper" title="Japanese paper">Japanese paper</a>, <span title="Japanese-language romanization"><i lang="ja-Latn">washi</i></span> <span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja-Hani">和紙</span></span>, and Japanese ink).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[23]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Korea">Korea</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Calligraphy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Korea">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Korean_calligraphy" title="Korean calligraphy">Korean calligraphy</a></div>
<p>The modern <a href="/wiki/Hangul" title="Hangul">Korean alphabet</a> and its use of the circle required the creation of a new technique not used in traditional Chinese calligraphy.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Vietnam">Vietnam</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Calligraphy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Vietnam">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Vietnamese_calligraphy" title="Vietnamese calligraphy">Vietnamese calligraphy</a></div>
<p>Vietnam abolished the <a href="/wiki/Ch%E1%BB%AF_N%C3%B4m" title="Chữ Nôm">Chữ Nôm</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ch%E1%BB%AF_H%C3%A1n" title="Chữ Hán">Chữ Hán</a>, an old Vietnamese writing system based on Chinese characters and replaced it with the Latin alphabet. However, the calligraphic traditions continue to be preserved.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[24]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Mongolia">Mongolia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Calligraphy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Mongolia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Mongolian_calligraphy" title="Mongolian calligraphy">Mongolian calligraphy</a></div>
<p>Mongolian calligraphy is also influenced by Chinese calligraphy, from tools to style.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2022)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Tibet">Tibet</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Calligraphy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Tibet">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Tibetan_calligraphy" title="Tibetan calligraphy">Tibetan calligraphy</a></div>
<p>Tibetan calligraphy is central to <a href="/wiki/Tibet" title="Tibet">Tibetan</a> culture. The script is derived from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Indic_script" title="Indic script">Indic scripts</a>. The nobles of Tibet, such as the High <a href="/wiki/Lama" title="Lama">Lamas</a> and inhabitants of the <a href="/wiki/Potala_Palace" title="Potala Palace">Potala Palace</a>, were usually capable calligraphers. <a href="/wiki/Tibet" title="Tibet">Tibet</a> has been a center of <a href="/wiki/Buddhism" title="Buddhism">Buddhism</a> for several centuries, and that religion places a great deal of significance on written word. This does not provide for a large body of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Secular" title="Secular">secular</a> pieces, although they do exist (but are usually related in some way to Tibetan Buddhism). Almost all high religious writing involved calligraphy, including letters sent by the <a href="/wiki/Dalai_Lama" title="Dalai Lama">Dalai Lama</a> and other religious and secular authority. Calligraphy is particularly evident on their <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Prayer_wheels" title="Prayer wheels">prayer wheels</a>, although this calligraphy was forged rather than scribed, much like Arab and Roman calligraphy is often found on buildings. Although originally done with a reed, Tibetan calligraphers now use chisel tipped pens and markers as well.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2022)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<h4><span id=".22Temporary_calligraphy.22"></span><span class="mw-headline" id='"Temporary_calligraphy"'>"Temporary calligraphy"</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Calligraphy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title='Edit section: "Temporary calligraphy"'>edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Temporary calligraphy, also called <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Water_calligraphy" title="Water calligraphy">water calligraphy</a>, is a practice of water-only calligraphy on the floor, which dries out within minutes. This practice is especially appreciated by the new generation of retired Chinese in public parks of China. These will often open studio-shops in tourist towns offering traditional Chinese calligraphy to tourists. Other than writing the clients name, they also sell fine brushes as souvenirs and limestone carved stamps.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2022)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>Since late 1980s, a few Chinese artists have branched out traditional Chinese calligraphy to a new territory by mingling <a href="/wiki/Chinese_characters" title="Chinese characters">Chinese characters</a> with English letters; notable new forms of calligraphy are <a href="/wiki/Xu_Bing" title="Xu Bing">Xu Bing</a>'s square calligraphy and DanNie's coolligraphy or cooligraphy.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2022)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Southeast_Asia_.28except_Vietnam.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Southeast_Asia_(except_Vietnam)">Southeast Asia (except Vietnam)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Calligraphy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Southeast Asia (except Vietnam)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1021810730" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="mod-gallery mod-gallery-default"><div class="title"><div>Caligraphy samples from South and South-East Asia (except Vietnam)</div></div><div class="main"><div><ul class="gallery mw-gallery-traditional nochecker bordered-images whitebg">

</p>

<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Philippines">Philippines</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Calligraphy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Philippines">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The Philippines has numerous ancient and indigenous scripts collectively called as <a href="/wiki/Suyat" title="Suyat">Suyat scripts</a>. Various ethno-linguistic groups in the Philippines prior to Spanish colonization in the 16th century up to the independence era in the 21st century have used the scripts with various mediums. By the end of colonialism, only four of the suyat scripts survived and continue to be used by certain communities in everyday life. These four scripts are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hanun%C3%B3%27o_alphabet" title="Hanunó'o alphabet">Hanunó'o/Hanunoo</a> of the Hanuno'o <a href="/wiki/Mangyan" title="Mangyan">Mangyan</a> people, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Buhid_alphabet" title="Buhid alphabet">Buhid/Buid</a> of the Buhid <a href="/wiki/Mangyan" title="Mangyan">Mangyan</a> people, <a href="/wiki/Tagbanwa_script" title="Tagbanwa script">Tagbanwa script</a> of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tagbanwa_people" title="Tagbanwa people">Tagbanwa people</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Palawan_people" title="Palawan people">Palaw'an/Pala'wan</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Palawan_people" title="Palawan people">Palaw'an people</a>. All four scripts were inscribed in the <a href="/wiki/UNESCO" title="UNESCO">UNESCO</a> <a href="/wiki/Memory_of_the_World_Programme" title="Memory of the World Programme">Memory of the World Programme</a>, under the name <i>Philippine Paleographs (Hanunoo, Buid, Tagbanua and Pala’wan)</i>, in 1999.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[25]</a></sup>
</p><p>Due to dissent from colonialism, many artists and cultural experts have revived the usage of <a href="/wiki/Suyat" title="Suyat">suyat</a> scripts that went extinct due to Spanish persecution. These scripts being revived include the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kulitan_alphabet" title="Kulitan alphabet">Kulitan</a> script of the <a href="/wiki/Kapampangan_language" title="Kapampangan language">Kapampangan people</a>, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Visayan_languages" title="Visayan languages">badlit</a> script of various <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Visayan_languages" title="Visayan languages">Visayan ethnic groups</a>, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eskaya" title="Eskaya">Iniskaya</a> script of the <a href="/wiki/Eskaya_people" title="Eskaya people">Eskaya people</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Baybayin" title="Baybayin">Baybayin</a> script of the <a href="/wiki/Tagalog_people" title="Tagalog people">Tagalog people</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Ilocano_language" title="Ilocano language">Kur-itan</a> script of the <a href="/wiki/Ilocano_people" title="Ilocano people">Ilocano people</a>, among many others.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[26]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[28]</a></sup> Due to the diversity of <a href="/wiki/Suyat" title="Suyat">suyat</a> scripts, all calligraphy written in <a href="/wiki/Suyat" title="Suyat">suyat</a> scripts are collectively called as Filipino suyat calligraphy, although each are distinct from each other.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[29]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cnnphilippines.com_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-cnnphilippines.com-31">[30]</a></sup> Calligraphy using the Western alphabet and the Arabic alphabet are also prevalent in the Philippines due to its colonial past, but the Western alphabet and the Arabic alphabet are not considered as suyat, and therefore Western-alphabet and Arabic calligraphy are not considered as suyat calligraphy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[31]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[32]</a></sup>
</p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="South_Asia">South Asia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Calligraphy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: South Asia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="India">India</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Calligraphy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: India">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Indian_calligraphy" title="Indian calligraphy">Indian calligraphy</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Religious_text" title="Religious text">Religious texts</a> are the most frequent purpose for Indian calligraphy. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Monastic" title="Monastic">Monastic</a> Buddhist communities had members trained in calligraphy and shared responsibility for duplicating sacred scriptures.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[33]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Jainism" title="Jainism">Jaina</a> traders incorporated illustrated manuscripts celebrating Jaina saints. These manuscripts were produced using inexpensive material, like palm leave and birch, with fine calligraphy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[34]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Nepal">Nepal</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Calligraphy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Nepal">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Nepalese calligraphy is primarily created using the <a href="/wiki/Ranjana_script" title="Ranjana script">Ranjana script</a>. The script itself, along with its derivatives (like <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lanydza_Script" title="Lanydza Script">Lantsa</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/%27Phags-pa_script" title="'Phags-pa script">Phagpa</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kutila" title="Kutila">Kutila</a>) are used in <a href="/wiki/Nepal" title="Nepal">Nepal</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tibet" title="Tibet">Tibet</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bhutan" title="Bhutan">Bhutan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Leh" title="Leh">Leh</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mongolia" title="Mongolia">Mongolia</a>, coastal Japan, and Korea to write "<a href="/wiki/Om_mani_padme_hum" title="Om mani padme hum">Om mani padme hum</a>" and other sacred <a href="/wiki/Buddhist_texts" title="Buddhist texts">Buddhist texts</a>, mainly those derived from <a href="/wiki/Sanskrit" title="Sanskrit">Sanskrit</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pali" title="Pali">Pali</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2022)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Africa">Africa</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Calligraphy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Africa">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Egypt">Egypt</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Calligraphy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Egypt">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p><a href="/wiki/Egyptian_hieroglyphs" title="Egyptian hieroglyphs">Egyptian hieroglyphs</a> were the formal <a href="/wiki/Writing_system" title="Writing system">writing system</a> used in <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Egypt" title="Ancient Egypt">Ancient Egypt</a>. Hieroglyphs combined <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Logograph" title="Logograph">logographic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Syllabary" title="Syllabary">syllabic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Alphabet" title="Alphabet">alphabetic</a> elements, with a total of some 1,000 distinct characters.
</p>
<h4><span id="Ethiopia.2FAbyssinia"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Ethiopia/Abyssinia">Ethiopia/Abyssinia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Calligraphy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Ethiopia/Abyssinia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ethiopian_Scroll_comprising_prayers_against_various_ailments_Wellcome_L0031387.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="6030" data-file-width="2908" decoding="async" height="353" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/39/Ethiopian_Scroll_comprising_prayers_against_various_ailments_Wellcome_L0031387.jpg/170px-Ethiopian_Scroll_comprising_prayers_against_various_ailments_Wellcome_L0031387.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/39/Ethiopian_Scroll_comprising_prayers_against_various_ailments_Wellcome_L0031387.jpg/255px-Ethiopian_Scroll_comprising_prayers_against_various_ailments_Wellcome_L0031387.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/39/Ethiopian_Scroll_comprising_prayers_against_various_ailments_Wellcome_L0031387.jpg/340px-Ethiopian_Scroll_comprising_prayers_against_various_ailments_Wellcome_L0031387.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ethiopian_Scroll_comprising_prayers_against_various_ailments_Wellcome_L0031387.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A painting of <a href="/wiki/Susenyos_I" title="Susenyos I">Susenyos I</a> (r. 1607–1632) on a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ge%27ez" title="Ge'ez">Ge'ez</a> prayer scroll meant to dispel evil spirits, <a href="/wiki/Wellcome_Collection" title="Wellcome Collection">Wellcome Collection</a>, London</div></div></div>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ethiopian" title="Ethiopian">Ethiopian</a> (Abyssinian) calligraphy began with the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ge%27ez_script" title="Ge'ez script">Ge'ez script</a>, which replaced <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Epigraphic_South_Arabian" title="Epigraphic South Arabian">Epigraphic South Arabian</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Aksum" title="Kingdom of Aksum">Kingdom of Aksum</a>, that was developed specifically for <a href="/wiki/Ethiopian_Semitic_languages" title="Ethiopian Semitic languages">Ethiopian Semitic languages</a>. In those languages that use it, such as <a href="/wiki/Amharic" title="Amharic">Amharic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tigrinya_language" title="Tigrinya language">Tigrinya</a>, the script is called <i><span title="Semitic languages romanization"><i lang="sem-Latn">Fidäl</i></span></i>, which means script or alphabet. The Epigraphic South Arabian letters were used for a few inscriptions into the 8th century, though not any South Arabian language since <a href="/wiki/D%CA%BFmt" title="Dʿmt">Dʿmt</a>.
</p><p>Early inscriptions in Ge'ez and Ge'ez script have been dated to as early as the 5th century BCE, and in a sort of proto-Ge'ez written in ESA since the 9th century BCE. Ge'ez literature begins with the Christianization of Ethiopia (and the civilization of Axum) in the 4th century, during the reign of <a href="/wiki/Ezana_of_Axum" title="Ezana of Axum">Ezana of Axum</a>.
</p><p>The Ge'ez script is read from left to right and has been adapted to write other languages, usually ones that are also Semitic. The most widespread use is for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Amharic_language" title="Amharic language">Amharic</a> in <a href="/wiki/Ethiopia" title="Ethiopia">Ethiopia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tigrinya_language" title="Tigrinya language">Tigrinya</a> in <a href="/wiki/Eritrea" title="Eritrea">Eritrea</a> and Ethiopia.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2022)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Americas">Americas</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Calligraphy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Americas">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Maya_civilization">Maya civilization</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Calligraphy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Maya civilization">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p><a href="/wiki/Maya_civilization" title="Maya civilization">Maya</a> calligraphy was expressed via <a href="/wiki/Maya_script" title="Maya script">Maya glyphs</a>; modern Maya calligraphy is mainly used on <a href="/wiki/Seal_(emblem)" title="Seal (emblem)">seals</a> and monuments in the <a href="/wiki/Yucat%C3%A1n_Peninsula" title="Yucatán Peninsula">Yucatán Peninsula</a> in Mexico. Maya glyphs are rarely used in government offices; however in <a href="/wiki/Campeche" title="Campeche">Campeche</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Yucat%C3%A1n_(state)" title="Yucatán (state)">Yucatán</a> and <a href="/wiki/Quintana_Roo" title="Quintana Roo">Quintana Roo</a>, calligraphy in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Maya_language" title="Maya language">Maya languages</a> is written in Latin script rather than Maya glyphs. Some commercial companies in southern Mexico use Maya glyphs as symbols of their business. Some community associations and modern Maya brotherhoods use Maya glyphs as symbols of their groups.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2022)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>Most of the archaeological sites in Mexico such as <a href="/wiki/Chichen_Itza" title="Chichen Itza">Chichen Itza</a>, Labna, <a href="/wiki/Uxmal" title="Uxmal">Uxmal</a>, <a href="/wiki/Edzna" title="Edzna">Edzna</a>, <a href="/wiki/Calakmul" title="Calakmul">Calakmul</a>, etc. have glyphs in their structures. Carved stone monuments known as <a href="/wiki/Stele" title="Stele">stele</a> are common sources of ancient Maya calligraphy.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2022)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Europe">Europe</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Calligraphy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Europe">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Western_calligraphy" title="Western calligraphy">Western calligraphy</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1021810730" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="mod-gallery mod-gallery-default"><div class="title"><div>Caligraphy samples from Europe and near East</div></div><div class="main"><div><ul class="gallery mw-gallery-traditional nochecker bordered-images whitebg">

</p>

<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 215px"><div style="width: 215px">

2x" width="180"/></a></div></div>
<div class="gallerytext">
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Georgian_calligraphy" title="Georgian calligraphy">Georgian calligraphy</a> is centuries-old tradition of an artistic writing of the <a href="/wiki/Georgian_language" title="Georgian language">Georgian language</a> with its <a href="/wiki/Georgian_scripts" title="Georgian scripts">three scripts</a>
</p>
</div>
</div></li>
<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 215px"><div style="width: 215px">
<div class="thumb" style="width: 210px;"><div style="margin:67.5px auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Westerncalligraphy.jpg" title="Modern Western calligraphy"><img alt="Modern Western calligraphy" data-file-height="166" data-file-width="400" decoding="async" height="75" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/Westerncalligraphy.jpg/180px-Westerncalligraphy.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/Westerncalligraphy.jpg/270px-Westerncalligraphy.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/Westerncalligraphy.jpg/360px-Westerncalligraphy.jpg 2x" width="180"/></a></div></div>
<div class="gallerytext">
<p>Modern Western calligraphy
</p>
</div>
</div></li>
</ul></div></div></div>
<p>Calligraphy in Europe recognizable in the use of the <a href="/wiki/Latin_script" title="Latin script">Latin script</a> in <a href="/wiki/Western_Europe" title="Western Europe">Western Europe</a>, and in the use of the <a href="/wiki/Greek_alphabet" title="Greek alphabet">Greek</a>, <a href="/wiki/Armenian_alphabet" title="Armenian alphabet">Armenian</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Georgian_scripts" title="Georgian scripts">Georgian</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Cyrillic_script" title="Cyrillic script">Cyrillic scripts</a> in <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Europe" title="Eastern Europe">Eastern Europe</a>.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Western_Europe">Western Europe</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Calligraphy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Western Europe">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Roman_Antiquity">Roman Antiquity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Calligraphy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Roman Antiquity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Latin_alphabet" title="Latin alphabet">Latin alphabet</a> appeared about 600 BCE in <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Rome" title="Ancient Rome">ancient Rome</a>, and by the first century CE it had developed into <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Roman_imperial_capitals" title="Roman imperial capitals">Roman imperial capitals</a> carved on stones, <a href="/wiki/Rustic_capitals" title="Rustic capitals">rustic capitals</a> painted on walls, and <a href="/wiki/Roman_cursive" title="Roman cursive">Roman cursive</a> for daily use. In the second and third centuries the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Uncial" title="Uncial">uncial</a> lettering style developed. As writing withdrew to monasteries, uncial script was found more suitable for copying the <a href="/wiki/Bible" title="Bible">Bible</a> and other religious texts. It was the monasteries which preserved calligraphic traditions during the fourth and fifth centuries, when the Roman Empire fell and Europe entered the <a href="/wiki/Early_Middle_Ages" title="Early Middle Ages">early Middle Ages</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[35]</a></sup>
</p><p>At the height of the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Empire" title="Roman Empire">Roman Empire</a>, its power reached as far as Great Britain; when the empire fell, its literary influence remained. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Semi-uncial" title="Semi-uncial">Semi-uncial</a> generated the Irish Semi-uncial, the small Anglo-Saxon.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[36]</a></sup> Each region developed its own standards following the main monastery of the region (i.e. <a href="/wiki/Merovingian_script" title="Merovingian script">Merovingian script</a>, <a href="/wiki/Merovingian_script#Laon" title="Merovingian script">Laon script</a>, <a href="/wiki/Merovingian_script#Luxeuil" title="Merovingian script">Luxeuil script</a>, <a href="/wiki/Visigothic_script" title="Visigothic script">Visigothic script</a>, <a href="/wiki/Beneventan_script" title="Beneventan script">Beneventan script</a>), which are mostly cursive and hardly readable.
</p>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Middle_Ages">Middle Ages</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Calligraphy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Middle Ages">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>
<p>Christian churches promoted the development of writing through the prolific copying of the Bible, the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Breviary" title="Roman Breviary">Breviary</a>, and other sacred texts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEde_Hamel2001a_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEde_Hamel2001a-38">[37]</a></sup> Two distinct styles of writing known as uncial and half-uncial (from the Latin <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">uncia</i></span>, or "inch") developed from a variety of Roman <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bookhand" title="Bookhand">bookhands</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[38]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources#What_information_to_include" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title="A complete citation is needed. (August 2022)">full citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> The 7th–9th centuries in northern Europe were the heyday of Celtic illuminated manuscripts, such as the Book of Durrow, <a href="/wiki/Lindisfarne_Gospels" title="Lindisfarne Gospels">Lindisfarne Gospels</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Book_of_Kells" title="Book of Kells">Book of Kells</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[39]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Charlemagne" title="Charlemagne">Charlemagne</a>'s devotion to improved scholarship resulted in the recruiting of "a crowd of scribes", according to <a href="/wiki/Alcuin" title="Alcuin">Alcuin</a>, the Abbot of <a href="/wiki/York" title="York">York</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[40]</a></sup> Alcuin developed the style known as the Caroline or <a href="/wiki/Carolingian_minuscule" title="Carolingian minuscule">Carolingian minuscule</a>. The first manuscript in this hand was the Godescalc Evangelistary (finished 783)—a Gospel book written by the scribe Godescalc.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[41]</a></sup> Carolingian remains the one progenitor hand from which modern booktype descends.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[42]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the eleventh century, the Caroline evolved into the <a href="/wiki/Blackletter" title="Blackletter">blackletter</a> ("Gothic") script, which was more compact and made it possible to fit more text on a page.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lovett2000_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-lovett2000-44">[43]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 72">: 72 </span></sup> The Gothic calligraphy styles became dominant throughout Europe; and in 1454, when <a href="/wiki/Johannes_Gutenberg" title="Johannes Gutenberg">Johannes Gutenberg</a> developed the first printing press in Mainz, Germany, he adopted the Gothic style, making it the first <a href="/wiki/Typeface" title="Typeface">typeface</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lovett2000_44-1"><a href="#cite_note-lovett2000-44">[43]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 141">: 141 </span></sup>
</p>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Renaissance">Renaissance</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Calligraphy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Renaissance">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>
<p>In the 15th century, the rediscovery of old Carolingian texts encouraged the creation of the <a href="/wiki/Humanist_minuscule" title="Humanist minuscule">humanist minuscule</a> or <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">littera antiqua</i></span>. The 17th century saw the <a href="/wiki/Bastarda" title="Bastarda">Batarde script</a> from France, and the 18th century saw the <a href="/wiki/English_script_(calligraphy)" title="English script (calligraphy)">English script</a> spread across Europe and world through their books.
</p><p>In the mid-1600s French officials, flooded with documents written in various hands and varied levels of skill, complained that many such documents were beyond their ability to decipher.  The Office of the Financier thereupon restricted all legal documents to three hands, namely the Coulee, the Rhonde, (known as <a href="/wiki/Round_hand" title="Round hand">Round hand</a> in English) and a Speed Hand sometimes simply called the <a href="/wiki/Bastarda" title="Bastarda">Bastarda</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Whalley_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-Whalley-45">[44]</a></sup>
</p><p>While there were many great French masters at the time, the most influential in proposing these hands was Louis Barbedor, who published <i>Les Ecritures Financière Et Italienne Bastarde Dans Leur Naturel</i> circa 1650.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Whalley_45-1"><a href="#cite_note-Whalley-45">[44]</a></sup>
</p><p>With the destruction of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Camera_Apostolica" title="Camera Apostolica">Camera Apostolica</a> during the <a href="/wiki/Sack_of_Rome_(1527)" title="Sack of Rome (1527)">sack of Rome (1527)</a>, the capitol for writing masters moved to Southern France. By 1600, the Italic Cursiva began to be replaced by a technological refinement, the Italic Chancery Circumflessa, which in turn fathered the Rhonde and later English <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Roundhand" title="Roundhand">Roundhand</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Whalley_45-2"><a href="#cite_note-Whalley-45">[44]</a></sup>
</p><p>In England, <a href="/wiki/Philip_Ayres_(poet)" title="Philip Ayres (poet)">Ayres</a> and Banson popularized the Round Hand while Snell is noted for his reaction to them, and warnings of restraint and proportionality. Still Edward Crocker began publishing his copybooks 40 years before the aforementioned.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Whalley_45-3"><a href="#cite_note-Whalley-45">[44]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Eastern_Europe">Eastern Europe</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Calligraphy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Eastern Europe">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Other European styles use the same tools and practices, but differ by character set and stylistic preferences.
For Slavonic lettering, the history of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Slavic_peoples" title="Slavic peoples">Slavonic</a> and consequently <a href="/wiki/Russia" title="Russia">Russian</a> <a href="/wiki/Writing_system" title="Writing system">writing systems</a> differs fundamentally from the one of the <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin language</a>. It evolved from the 10th century to today.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Style">Style</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Calligraphy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Style">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Unlike a typeface, handwritten calligraphy is characterised by irregularity in the characters which vary in size, shape, style, and color, producing a distinct aesthetic value, although it may also make the content illegible to readers. As with <a href="/wiki/Chinese_calligraphy" title="Chinese calligraphy">Chinese</a> or <a href="/wiki/Islamic_calligraphy" title="Islamic calligraphy">Islamic calligraphy</a>, Western calligraphic script employed the use of strict rules and shapes. Quality writing had a rhythm and regularity to the letters, with a "geometrical" order of the lines on the page. Each character had, and often still has, a precise <a href="/wiki/Stroke_order" title="Stroke order">stroke order</a>.
</p><p>Sacred <a href="/wiki/Western_calligraphy" title="Western calligraphy">Western calligraphy</a> has some unique features, such as the illumination of the first letter of each book or chapter in medieval times. A decorative "carpet page" may precede the literature, filled with ornate, geometrical depictions of bold-hued animals. The <a href="/wiki/Lindisfarne_Gospels" title="Lindisfarne Gospels">Lindisfarne Gospels</a> (715–720 CE) are an early example.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[45]</a></sup> Many of the themes and variations of today's contemporary Western calligraphy are found in the pages of <a href="/wiki/The_Saint_John%27s_Bible" title="The Saint John's Bible">The Saint John's Bible</a>. A particularly modern example is <a href="/wiki/Timothy_Botts" title="Timothy Botts">Timothy Botts</a>' illustrated edition of the Bible, with 360 calligraphic images as well as a calligraphy <a href="/wiki/Typeface" title="Typeface">typeface</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[46]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Islamic_world">Islamic world</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Calligraphy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Islamic world">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Islamic_calligraphy" title="Islamic calligraphy">Islamic calligraphy</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1021810730" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="mod-gallery mod-gallery-default"><div class="title"><div>Samples of Islamic caligraphy samples</div></div><div class="main"><div><ul class="gallery mw-gallery-traditional nochecker bordered-images whitebg">
