<p>Islamic calligraphy<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[b]</a></sup> has evolved alongside <a href="/wiki/Islam" title="Islam">Islam</a> and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arabic_language" title="Arabic language">Arabic language</a>. As it is based on Arabic letters, some call it "Arabic calligraphy". However the term "Islamic calligraphy" is a more appropriate term as it comprises all works of calligraphy by Muslim calligraphers of different national cultures, like <a href="/wiki/Persian_calligraphy" title="Persian calligraphy">Persian</a> or <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Empire" title="Ottoman Empire">Ottoman</a> calligraphy, from <a href="/wiki/Al-Andalus" title="Al-Andalus">Al-Andalus</a> in medieval <a href="/wiki/Spain" title="Spain">Spain</a> to China.
</p><p>Islamic calligraphy is associated with geometric Islamic art (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arabesque_(Islamic_art)" title="Arabesque (Islamic art)">arabesque</a>) on the walls and ceilings of <a href="/wiki/Mosque" title="Mosque">mosques</a> as well as on the page or other materials. Contemporary artists in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Islamic_world" title="Islamic world">Islamic world</a> may draw on the heritage of calligraphy to create modern calligraphic inscriptions, like <a href="/wiki/Logo" title="Logo">corporate logos</a>, or abstractions.
</p><p>Instead of recalling something related to the spoken word, calligraphy for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Muslim" title="Muslim">Muslims</a> is a visible expression of the highest art of all, the art of the <a href="/wiki/Spirituality" title="Spirituality">spiritual</a> world. Calligraphy has arguably become the most venerated form of Islamic art because it provides a link between the languages of the Muslims with the religion of Islam. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Qur%27an" title="Qur'an">Qur'an</a> has played an important role in the development and evolution of the Arabic language, and by extension, calligraphy in the Arabic alphabet. Proverbs and passages from the Qur'an are still sources for Islamic calligraphy.
</p><p>During the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ottoman_era" title="Ottoman era">Ottoman civilization</a>, Islamic calligraphy attained special prominence. The city of Istanbul is an open exhibition hall for all kinds and varieties of calligraphy, from inscriptions in mosques to fountains, schools, houses, etc.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2022)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Persia">Persia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Calligraphy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Persia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Persian_calligraphy" title="Persian calligraphy">Persian calligraphy</a></div>
<p>Persian calligraphy has been present in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Persia" title="Persia">Persian</a> region before <a href="/wiki/Islam" title="Islam">Islamisation</a>. In <a href="/wiki/Zoroastrianism" title="Zoroastrianism">Zoroastrianism</a>, beautiful and clear writings were always praised.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2022)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Antiquity">Antiquity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Calligraphy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: Antiquity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>It is believed<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Words_to_watch#Unsupported_attributions" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Words to watch"><span title="The material near this tag may use weasel words or too-vague attribution. (April 2022)">by whom?</span></a></i>]</sup> that <a href="/wiki/Old_Persian" title="Old Persian">ancient Persian script</a> was invented by about 600–500 BCE to provide monument inscriptions for the <a href="/wiki/Achaemenid_Empire#Achaemenid_kings_and_rulers" title="Achaemenid Empire">Achaemenid kings</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2022)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> These scripts consisted of horizontal, vertical, and diagonal nail-shape letters, which is why it is called <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cuneiform_script" title="Cuneiform script">cuneiform script</a> (lit. "script of nails") (<i><span title="Persian-language romanization"><i lang="fa-Latn">khat-e-mikhi</i></span></i>) in <a href="/wiki/Persian_language" title="Persian language">Persian</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Writing_better_articles#Stay_on_topic" title="Wikipedia:Writing better articles"><span title="The material near this tag may contain information that is not relevant to the article's main topic. Did ancient Iranians actually use cuneiform specifically for calligraphy? (August 2022)">relevant?</span></a></i>]</sup> Centuries later, other scripts such as "<a href="/wiki/Pahlavi_scripts" title="Pahlavi scripts">Pahlavi</a>" and "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Avestan_script" title="Avestan script">Avestan</a>" scripts were used in ancient Persia. Pahlavi was a middle Persian script developed from the Aramaic script and became the official script of the Sassanian empire (224–651 CE).<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2022)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Contemporary_scripts">Contemporary scripts</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Calligraphy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=36" title="Edit section: Contemporary scripts">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nasta%27liq_script" title="Nasta'liq script">Nasta'liq</a> style is the most popular contemporary style among classical Persian calligraphy scripts;<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2022)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Persian calligraphers call it the "bride of calligraphy scripts". This calligraphy style has been based on such a strong structure that it has changed very little since <a href="/wiki/Mir_Ali_Tabrizi" title="Mir Ali Tabrizi">Mir Ali Tabrizi</a> had found the optimum composition of the letters and graphical rules.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2022)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> It has just been fine-tuned during the past seven centuries.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title='So what makes it a "contemporary" script if it is seven centuries old? (April 2022)'>clarification needed</span></a></i>]</sup>  It has very strict rules for graphical shape of the letters and for combination of the letters, words, and composition of the whole calligraphy piece.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2022)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Modern_calligraphy">Modern calligraphy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Calligraphy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=37" title="Edit section: Modern calligraphy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Revival">Revival</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Calligraphy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=38" title="Edit section: Revival">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>After <a href="/wiki/Printing" title="Printing">printing</a> became ubiquitous from the 15th century onward, the production of illuminated manuscripts began to decline.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEde_Hamel2001a_38-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEde_Hamel2001a-38">[37]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[47]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources#What_information_to_include" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title="A complete citation is needed. (August 2022)">full citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> However, the rise of printing did not mean the end of calligraphy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEde_Hamel2001a_38-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEde_Hamel2001a-38">[37]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEZapf2007_4-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEZapf2007-4">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[48]</a></sup> A clear distinction between handwriting and more elaborate forms of lettering and script began to make its way into manuscripts and books at the beginning of the 16th century.
</p><p>The modern revival of calligraphy began at the end of the 19th century, influenced by the aesthetics and philosophy of <a href="/wiki/William_Morris" title="William Morris">William Morris</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Arts_and_Crafts_movement" title="Arts and Crafts movement">Arts and Crafts movement</a>. <a href="/wiki/Edward_Johnston" title="Edward Johnston">Edward Johnston</a> is regarded as being the father of modern calligraphy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ejf_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-ejf-51">[49]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[50]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[51]</a></sup> After studying published copies of manuscripts by architect <a href="/wiki/William_Harrison_Cowlishaw" title="William Harrison Cowlishaw">William Harrison Cowlishaw</a>, he was introduced to <a href="/wiki/William_Lethaby" title="William Lethaby">William Lethaby</a> in 1898, principal of the <a href="/wiki/Central_School_of_Art_and_Design" title="Central School of Art and Design">Central School of Arts and Crafts</a>, who advised him to study manuscripts at the <a href="/wiki/British_Museum" title="British Museum">British Museum</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[c]</a></sup>
</p><p>This triggered Johnston's interest in the art of calligraphy with the use of a broad-edged pen. He began a teaching course in calligraphy at the Central School in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Southampton_Row" title="Southampton Row">Southampton Row</a>, London from September 1899, where he influenced the typeface designer and sculptor <a href="/wiki/Eric_Gill" title="Eric Gill">Eric Gill</a>. He was commissioned by  <a href="/wiki/Frank_Pick" title="Frank Pick">Frank Pick</a> to design a <a href="/wiki/Johnston_(typeface)" title="Johnston (typeface)">new typeface</a> for <a href="/wiki/London_Underground" title="London Underground">London Underground</a>, still used today (with minor modifications).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[52]</a></sup>
</p><p>He has been credited for reviving the art of modern <a href="/wiki/Penmanship" title="Penmanship">penmanship</a> and lettering single-handedly through his books and teachings – his handbook on the subject, <i>Writing &amp; Illuminating, &amp; Lettering</i> (1906) was particularly influential on a generation of British typographers and calligraphers, including <a href="/wiki/Graily_Hewitt" title="Graily Hewitt">Graily Hewitt</a>, <a href="/wiki/Stanley_Morison" title="Stanley Morison">Stanley Morison</a>, <a href="/wiki/Eric_Gill" title="Eric Gill">Eric Gill</a>, <a href="/wiki/Alfred_Fairbank" title="Alfred Fairbank">Alfred Fairbank</a> and Anna Simons. Johnston also devised the simply crafted round calligraphic handwriting style, written with a broad pen, known today as the <i>Foundational hand</i>. Johnston initially taught his students an uncial hand using a flat pen angle, but later taught his hand using a slanted pen angle.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[53]</a></sup> He first referred to this hand as "Foundational Hand" in his 1909 publication, <i>Manuscript &amp; Inscription Letters for Schools and Classes and for the Use of Craftsmen</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[54]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Subsequent_developments">Subsequent developments</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Calligraphy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=39" title="Edit section: Subsequent developments">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Graily_Hewitt" title="Graily Hewitt">Graily Hewitt</a> taught at the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Central_School_of_Arts_and_Crafts" title="Central School of Arts and Crafts">Central School of Arts and Crafts</a> and published together with Johnston throughout the early part of the century. Hewitt was central to the revival of <a href="/wiki/Gilding" title="Gilding">gilding</a> in calligraphy, and his prolific output on type design also appeared between 1915 and 1943. He is attributed with the  revival of  gilding with <a href="/wiki/Gesso" title="Gesso">gesso</a> and gold leaf on <a href="/wiki/Vellum" title="Vellum">vellum</a>. Hewitt helped to found the <a href="/wiki/Society_of_Scribes_%26_Illuminators" title="Society of Scribes &amp; Illuminators">Society of Scribes &amp; Illuminators</a> (SSI) in 1921, probably the world's foremost calligraphy society.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Graily_Hewitt.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1779" data-file-width="1646" decoding="async" height="238" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/Graily_Hewitt.JPG/220px-Graily_Hewitt.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/Graily_Hewitt.JPG/330px-Graily_Hewitt.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/Graily_Hewitt.JPG/440px-Graily_Hewitt.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Graily_Hewitt.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>An example of <a href="/wiki/Graily_Hewitt" title="Graily Hewitt">Graily Hewitt</a>'s calligraphy</div></div></div>
<p>Hewitt is not without both critics<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[55]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources#What_information_to_include" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title="A complete citation is needed. (August 2022)">full citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> and supporters<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[56]</a></sup> in his rendering of <a href="/wiki/Cennino_Cennini" title="Cennino Cennini">Cennino Cennini</a>'s medieval gesso recipes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[57]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Donald_Jackson_(calligrapher)" title="Donald Jackson (calligrapher)">Donald Jackson</a>, a British calligrapher, has sourced his gesso recipes from earlier centuries a number of which are not presently in English translation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[58]</a></sup> Graily Hewitt created the patent announcing the award to <a href="/wiki/Prince_Philip,_Duke_of_Edinburgh" title="Prince Philip, Duke of Edinburgh">Prince Philip</a> of the title of Duke of Edinburgh on November 19, 1947, the day before his marriage to Queen Elizabeth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[59]</a></sup>
</p><p>Johnston's pupil, Anna Simons, was instrumental in sparking off interest in calligraphy in Germany with her German translation of <i>Writing and Illuminating, and Lettering</i> in 1910.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ejf_51-1"><a href="#cite_note-ejf-51">[49]</a></sup> Austrian Rudolf Larisch, a teacher of lettering at the Vienna School of Art, published six lettering books that greatly influenced German-speaking calligraphers. Because German-speaking countries had not abandoned the Gothic hand in printing, Gothic also had a powerful effect on their styles.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Rudolf_Koch" title="Rudolf Koch">Rudolf Koch</a> was a friend and younger contemporary of Larisch. Koch's books, type designs, and teaching made him one of the most influential calligraphers of the 20th century in northern Europe and later in the U.S. Larisch and Koch taught and inspired many European calligraphers, notably <a href="/wiki/Karlgeorg_Hoefer" title="Karlgeorg Hoefer">Karlgeorg Hoefer</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Hermann_Zapf" title="Hermann Zapf">Hermann Zapf</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[60]</a></sup>
</p><p>Contemporary typefaces used by computers, from word processors like <a href="/wiki/Microsoft_Word" title="Microsoft Word">Microsoft Word</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Apple_Pages" title="Apple Pages">Apple Pages</a> to professional design software packages like <a href="/wiki/Adobe_InDesign" title="Adobe InDesign">Adobe InDesign</a>, owe a considerable debt to the past and to a small number of professional typeface designers today.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mediaville1996_1-3"><a href="#cite_note-mediaville1996-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEZapf2007_4-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEZapf2007-4">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[61]</a></sup>
</p>

<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r998391716">.mw-parser-output .div-col{margin-top:0.3em;column-width:30em}.mw-parser-output .div-col-small{font-size:90%}.mw-parser-output .div-col-rules{column-rule:1px solid #aaa}.mw-parser-output .div-col dl,.mw-parser-output .div-col ol,.mw-parser-output .div-col ul{margin-top:0}.mw-parser-output .div-col li,.mw-parser-output .div-col dd{page-break-inside:avoid;break-inside:avoid-column}</style><div class="div-col" style="column-width: 30em;">
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Asemic_writing" title="Asemic writing">Asemic writing</a> – Wordless open semantic form of writing</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Bastarda" title="Bastarda">Bastarda</a> – Blackletter script used in France and Germany</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Blackletter" title="Blackletter">Blackletter</a> – Historic European script and typeface</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Book_hand" title="Book hand">Book hand</a> – Legible handwriting style</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Brahmi_script" title="Brahmi script">Brāhmī script</a> – Ancient script of Central and South Asia</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Calligraffiti" title="Calligraffiti">Calligraffiti</a> – Calligraphy/typography/graffiti art form</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Chancery_hand" title="Chancery hand">Chancery hand</a> – Any of several styles  of historic handwriting</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Concrete_poetry" title="Concrete poetry">Concrete poetry</a> – Genre of poetry with lines arranged as a shape</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Court_hand" title="Court hand">Court hand</a> – Style of handwriting used in medieval English law courts</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Cursive" title="Cursive">Cursive</a> – Style of penmanship in which characters are written joined together in a flowing manner</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Handwriting" title="Handwriting">Handwriting</a> – Writing created by a person with a writing implement</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/History_of_writing" title="History of writing">History of writing</a> – Creation and development of permanent, physical records of language</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Italic_script" title="Italic script">Italic script</a> – Semi-cursive, slightly sloped style of handwriting and calligraphy developed in Italy</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/List_of_calligraphers" title="List of calligraphers">List of calligraphers</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Mathematical_Alphanumeric_Symbols" title="Mathematical Alphanumeric Symbols">Mathematical Alphanumeric Symbols</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Micrography" title="Micrography">Micrography</a> – Art genre using minute Hebrew letters</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Palaeography" title="Palaeography">Palaeography</a> – Study of historic handwriting</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Penmanship" title="Penmanship">Penmanship</a> – Technique of writing with the hand</li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ronde_script_(calligraphy)" title="Ronde script (calligraphy)">Ronde script (calligraphy)</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Rotunda_(script)" title="Rotunda (script)">Rotunda (script)</a> – Medieval blackletter script</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Round_hand" title="Round hand">Round hand</a> – Type of handwriting</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Secretary_hand" title="Secretary hand">Secretary hand</a> – Style of European handwriting</li>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Calligraphy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=41" title="Edit section: Notes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1011085734">.mw-parser-output .reflist{font-size:90%;margin-bottom:0.5em;list-style-type:decimal}.mw-parser-output .reflist .references{font-size:100%;margin-bottom:0;list-style-type:inherit}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns-2{column-width:30em}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns-3{column-width:25em}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns{margin-top:0.3em}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns ol{margin-top:0}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns li{page-break-inside:avoid;break-inside:avoid-column}.mw-parser-output .reflist-upper-alpha{list-style-type:upper-alpha}.mw-parser-output .reflist-upper-roman{list-style-type:upper-roman}.mw-parser-output .reflist-lower-alpha{list-style-type:lower-alpha}.mw-parser-output .reflist-lower-greek{list-style-type:lower-greek}.mw-parser-output .reflist-lower-roman{list-style-type:lower-roman}</style><div class="reflist reflist-lower-alpha">
<div class="mw-references-wrap"><ol class="references">
<li id="cite_note-13"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-13">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh-TW">書</span></span> (Taiwanese) being here used as in <span title="Yue Chinese-language text"><span lang="yue">楷书</span></span> (Cantonese) or <span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh-TW">楷書</span></span> (Taiwanese), meaning "writing style".<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="The text near this tag may need clarification or removal of jargon. (October 2013)">clarification needed</span></a></i>]</sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-48"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-48">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><i>Calligraphy</i> in Arabic is <i><span title="Arabic-language romanization"><i lang="ar-Latn">khatt ul-yad</i></span></i> (<span title="Arabic-language text"><span dir="rtl" lang="ar">خط اليد</span></span>) and in Persian is <span title="Persian-language romanization"><i lang="fa-Latn">Khosh-Nevisi</i></span> (<span title="Persian-language text"><span dir="rtl" lang="fa">خوشنویسی</span></span>.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-54"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-54">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Such as the Ramsey Psalter, BL, Harley MS 2904</span>
</li>
</ol></div></div>

<li id="cite_note-22"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-22">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation book cs1 cs1-prop-script cs1-prop-foreign-lang-source"><a class="external text" href="http://ctext.org/library.pl?if=en&amp;res=77358" rel="nofollow"><bdi lang="zh">康熙字典</bdi></a> [<i>Kangxi Zidian</i>] (in Chinese). 1716. p. 41.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=%E5%BA%B7%E7%86%99%E5%AD%97%E5%85%B8&amp;rft.pages=41&amp;rft.date=1716&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fctext.org%2Flibrary.pl%3Fif%3Den%26res%3D77358&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ACalligraphy"></span>. See, for example, the radicals <big><span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">卩</span></span></big>, <big><span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">厂</span></span></big>, or <big><span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">广</span></span></big>. The 2007 common shape for those characters does not clearly show the stroke order, but old versions, visible on p. 41, clearly allow the stroke order to be determined.</span>
